"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ab_initio_methods_(nuclear_physics)"" title=""Ab initio methods (nuclear physics)"">ab initio</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A mathematical model which seeks to describe <a href=""#nucleus""><span title=""See entry on this page at § nucleus"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">atomic nuclei</span></a> by solving the non-relativistic <a href=""#schrödinger_equation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § Schrödinger equation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">Schrödinger equation</span></a> for all constituent <a href=""#nucleon""><span title=""See entry on this page at § nucleon"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">nucleons</span></a> and the <a href=""#nuclear_force""><span title=""See entry on this page at § nuclear force"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">forces</span></a> that exist between them. Such methods yield precise results for very light nuclei but become more approximate for heavier nuclei.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Abbe_number"" title=""Abbe number"">Abbe number</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called the <b>V-number</b> or <b>constringence</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">In <a href=""#optics""><span title=""See entry on this page at § optics"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">optics</span></a> and lens design, a measure of a transparent material's <a href=""#dispersion""><span title=""See entry on this page at § dispersion"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">dispersion</span></a> (a variation of <a href=""#refractive_index""><span title=""See entry on this page at § refractive index"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">refractive index</span></a> versus <a href=""#wavelength""><span title=""See entry on this page at § wavelength"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">wavelength</span></a>). High values of V indicate low dispersion.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Absolute_electrode_potential"" title=""Absolute electrode potential"">absolute electrode potential</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrochemistry"" title=""Electrochemistry"">electrochemistry</a>, the electrode potential of a metal measured with respect to a universal reference system (without any additional metal–solution interface).</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Absolute_humidity"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Absolute humidity"">absolute humidity</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The ratio of the water vapor in a sample of air to the <a href=""#volume""><span title=""See entry on this page at § volume"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">volume</span></a> of the sample.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Absolute_magnitude"" title=""Absolute magnitude"">absolute magnitude</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Is a measure of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Luminosity"" title=""Luminosity"">luminosity</a> of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Celestial_object"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Celestial object"">celestial object</a>, on a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logarithmic_scale"" title=""Logarithmic scale"">logarithmic</a> <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnitude_(astronomy)"" title=""Magnitude (astronomy)"">astronomical magnitude</a> scale. An object's absolute magnitude is defined to be equal to the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Apparent_magnitude"" title=""Apparent magnitude"">apparent magnitude</a> that the object would have if it were viewed from a distance of exactly 10&#160;<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parsecs"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Parsecs"">parsecs</a> (32.6 <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Light-year"" title=""Light-year"">light-years</a>), with no <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extinction_(astronomy)"" title=""Extinction (astronomy)"">extinction</a> (or dimming) of its light due to absorption by <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cosmic_dust"" title=""Cosmic dust"">interstellar dust</a> particles.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Absolute_motion"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Absolute motion"">absolute motion</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p>Looks like this term doesn't have a definition yet.</p>

"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Absolute_pressure"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Absolute pressure"">absolute pressure</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Is zero-referenced against a perfect vacuum, using an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Absolute_scale"" title=""Absolute scale"">absolute scale</a>, so it is equal to gauge pressure plus atmospheric pressure.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Absolute_scale"" title=""Absolute scale"">absolute scale</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Is a system of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Measurement"" title=""Measurement"">measurement</a> that begins at a minimum, or zero point, and progresses in only one direction. An absolute scale differs from an arbitrary, or ""relative,"" scale, which begins at some point selected by a person and can progress in both directions. An absolute scale begins at a natural minimum, leaving only one direction in which to progress.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Absolute_zero"" title=""Absolute zero"">absolute zero</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The theoretical lowest possible <a href=""#temperature""><span title=""See entry on this page at § temperature"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">temperature</span></a>, understood by international agreement as equivalent to 0 <a href=""#kelvin""><span title=""See entry on this page at § Kelvin"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">Kelvin</span></a> or −273.15&#160;°C (−459.67&#160;°F). More formally, it is the theoretical lower limit of the <a href=""#thermodynamic_temperature""><span title=""See entry on this page at § thermodynamic temperature"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">thermodynamic temperature</span></a> scale, at which <a href=""#enthalpy""><span title=""See entry on this page at § enthalpy"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">enthalpy</span></a> and <a href=""#entropy""><span title=""See entry on this page at § entropy"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">entropy</span></a> of a cooled <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ideal_gas"" title=""Ideal gas"">ideal gas</a> reach their minimum values and the fundamental particles of nature have minimal vibrational motion.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Absorption_spectroscopy"" title=""Absorption spectroscopy"">absorption spectroscopy</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Refers to <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spectroscopy"" title=""Spectroscopy"">spectroscopic</a> techniques that measure the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Absorption_(electromagnetic_radiation)"" title=""Absorption (electromagnetic radiation)"">absorption</a> of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation"" title=""Electromagnetic radiation"">radiation</a>, as a function of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frequency"" title=""Frequency"">frequency</a> or <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wavelength"" title=""Wavelength"">wavelength</a>, due to its interaction with a sample. The sample absorbs energy, i.e., photons, from the radiating field. The intensity of the absorption varies as a function of frequency, and this variation is the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Absorption_spectroscopy#Absorption_spectrum"" title=""Absorption spectroscopy"">absorption spectrum</a>. Absorption spectroscopy is performed across the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electromagnetic_spectrum"" title=""Electromagnetic spectrum"">electromagnetic spectrum</a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Absorbance"" title=""Absorbance"">absorptivity</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p>Looks like this term doesn't have a definition yet.</p>

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Accelerating_expansion_of_the_universe"" title=""Accelerating expansion of the universe"">accelerating expansion of the universe</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Is the observation that the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Metric_expansion_of_space"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Metric expansion of space"">expansion</a> of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Universe"" title=""Universe"">universe</a> is such that the velocity at which a distant galaxy is receding from the observer is continuously increasing with time.<sup id=""cite_ref-NYT-20170220_1-0"" class=""reference""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#cite_note-NYT-20170220-1"">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-AM-20170818_2-0"" class=""reference""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#cite_note-AM-20170818-2"">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-ES-20180311_3-0"" class=""reference""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#cite_note-ES-20180311-3"">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-4"" class=""reference""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#cite_note-4"">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup></dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acceleration"" title=""Acceleration"">acceleration</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The rate at which the <a href=""#velocity""><span title=""See entry on this page at § velocity"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">velocity</span></a> of a body changes with time.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravitational_acceleration"" title=""Gravitational acceleration"">acceleration due to gravity</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The acceleration on an object caused by the force of <a href=""#gravitation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § gravitation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">gravitation</span></a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Accelerometer"" title=""Accelerometer"">accelerometer</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">An instrument used to measure the proper <a href=""#acceleration""><span title=""See entry on this page at § acceleration"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">acceleration</span></a> of a body irrespective of other forces.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acoustics"" title=""Acoustics"">acoustics</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The branch of physics dealing with the production, transmission and effects of <a href=""#sound""><span title=""See entry on this page at § sound"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">sound</span></a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adhesion"" title=""Adhesion"">adhesion</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The tendency of dissimilar particles or surfaces to cling to one another. Contrast <i><a href=""#cohesion""><span title=""See entry on this page at § cohesion"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">cohesion</span></a></i>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adiabatic_cooling"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Adiabatic cooling"">adiabatic cooling</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p>Looks like this term doesn't have a definition yet.</p>

"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adiabatic_heating"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Adiabatic heating"">adiabatic heating</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|""
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Adiabatic_process"" title=""Adiabatic process"">adiabatic process</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Occurs without transfer of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heat"" title=""Heat"">heat</a> or <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mass"" title=""Mass"">mass</a> of substances between a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermodynamic_system"" title=""Thermodynamic system"">thermodynamic system</a> and its surroundings. In an adiabatic process, energy is transferred to the surroundings only as <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Work_(thermodynamics)"" title=""Work (thermodynamics)"">work</a>.<sup id=""cite_ref-Carathéodory_5-0"" class=""reference""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#cite_note-Carathéodory-5"">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-Bailyn_21_6-0"" class=""reference""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#cite_note-Bailyn_21-6"">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> The adiabatic process provides a rigorous conceptual basis for the theory used to expound the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/First_law_of_thermodynamics"" title=""First law of thermodynamics"">first law of thermodynamics</a>, and as such it is a key concept in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermodynamics"" title=""Thermodynamics"">thermodynamics</a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aerodynamics"" title=""Aerodynamics"">aerodynamics</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Is the study of the motion of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Air"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Air"">air</a>, particularly its interaction with a solid object, such as an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Airplane"" title=""Airplane"">airplane</a> wing. It is a sub-field of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fluid_dynamics"" title=""Fluid dynamics"">fluid dynamics</a> and <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gas_dynamics"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Gas dynamics"">gas dynamics</a>, and many aspects of aerodynamics theory are common to these fields. </dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afocal_system"" title=""Afocal system"">afocal system</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Optics"" title=""Optics"">optics</a> an <b>afocal system</b> (a system without focus) is an optical system that produces no net convergence or divergence of the beam, i.e. has an infinite <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Effective_focal_length"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Effective focal length"">effective focal length</a>.<sup id=""cite_ref-7"" class=""reference""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#cite_note-7"">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> This type of system can be created with a pair of optical elements where the distance between the elements is equal to the sum of each element's <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Focal_length"" title=""Focal length"">focal length</a> (<i>d</i> = <i>f</i><sub>1</sub>+<i>f</i><sub>2</sub>).</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Air_mass_(astronomy)"" title=""Air mass (astronomy)"">air mass (astronomy)</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomy"" title=""Astronomy"">astronomy</a>, <b>air mass</b> or <b>airmass</b> is the ""amount of air that one is looking through""<sup id=""cite_ref-8"" class=""reference""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#cite_note-8"">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> when seeing a star or other celestial source below <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Earth&#39;s atmosphere"">Earth's atmosphere</a>. It is formulated as the integral of air density along the light <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ray_(optics)"" title=""Ray (optics)"">ray</a>.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Air_mass"" title=""Air mass"">air mass (meteorology)</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meteorology"" title=""Meteorology"">meteorology</a>, an <b>air mass</b> is a volume of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Air"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Air"">air</a> defined by its <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Temperature"" title=""Temperature"">temperature</a> and <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Water_vapor"" title=""Water vapor"">water vapor</a> content.  Air masses cover many hundreds or thousands of miles and adapt to the characteristics of the surface below them. They are classified according to latitude and their continental or maritime source regions.  Colder air masses are termed polar or arctic, while warmer air masses are deemed tropical. Continental and superior air masses are dry while maritime and monsoon air masses are moist.   <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Weather_front"" title=""Weather front"">Weather fronts</a> separate air masses with different density (temperature and/or moisture) characteristics. Once an air mass moves away from its source region, underlying vegetation and water bodies can quickly modify its character. Classification schemes tackle an air mass' characteristics, as well as modification.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Air_mass_(solar_energy)"" title=""Air mass (solar energy)"">air mass coefficient</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Defines the direct optical path length through the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Earth&#39;s atmosphere"">Earth's atmosphere</a>, expressed as a ratio relative to the path length vertically upwards, i.e. at the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zenith"" title=""Zenith"">zenith</a>.
The air mass coefficient can be used to help characterize the solar spectrum after <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_radiation"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Solar radiation"">solar radiation</a> has traveled through the atmosphere.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Albedo"" title=""Albedo"">albedo</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The fraction of the total <a href=""#light""><span title=""See entry on this page at § light"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">light</span></a> incident on a reflecting surface, especially a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Celestial_body"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Celestial body"">celestial body</a>, which is reflected back in all directions.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alloy"" title=""Alloy"">alloy</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A chemical mixture of a metal with one or more other metals or other elements.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alpha_decay"" title=""Alpha decay"">alpha decay</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Or <b>α-decay</b>, is a type of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radioactivity"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Radioactivity"">radioactive decay</a> in which an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atomic_nucleus"" title=""Atomic nucleus"">atomic nucleus</a> emits an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alpha_particle"" title=""Alpha particle"">alpha particle</a> (helium nucleus) and thereby transforms or 'decays' into a different atomic nucleus, with a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mass_number"" title=""Mass number"">mass number</a> that is reduced by four and an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atomic_number"" title=""Atomic number"">atomic number</a> that is reduced by two. An alpha particle is identical to the nucleus of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Helium-4"" title=""Helium-4"">helium-4</a> atom, which consists of two <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proton"" title=""Proton"">protons</a> and two <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neutron"" title=""Neutron"">neutrons</a>. It has a charge of <span class=""nowrap""><span data-sort-value=""6981320435297400000♠""></span>+2&#160;<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elementary_charge"" title=""Elementary charge""><i>e</i></a></span> and a mass of <span class=""nowrap""><span data-sort-value=""7000400000000000000♠""></span>4&#160;<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unified_atomic_mass_unit"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Unified atomic mass unit"">u</a></span>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alpha_particle"" title=""Alpha particle"">alpha particle</a> (α) (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also symbolized by <b>α<sup>2+</sup></b>, <b><span class=""chemf nowrap"">He<span style=""display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left""><sup style=""font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"">2+</sup><br /><sub style=""font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline""></sub></span></span></b>, and <b><span style=""white-space:nowrap;""><span style=""display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right""><sup style=""font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"">4</sup><br /><sub style=""font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"">2</sub></span>He<span style=""display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left""><sup style=""font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"">2+</sup><br /><sub style=""font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline""></sub></span></span></b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">A type of <a href=""#subatomic_particle""><span title=""See entry on this page at § subatomic particle"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">subatomic particle</span></a> consisting of two <a href=""#proton""><span title=""See entry on this page at § proton"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">protons</span></a> and two <a href=""#neutron""><span title=""See entry on this page at § neutron"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">neutrons</span></a> bound together into a particle identical to the <a href=""#atomic_nucleus""><span title=""See entry on this page at § atomic nucleus"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">nucleus</span></a> of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Helium-4"" title=""Helium-4"">helium-4</a> <a href=""#ion""><span title=""See entry on this page at § ion"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">ion</span></a>. Alpha particles are classically produced in the process of <a href=""#radioactive_decay""><span title=""See entry on this page at § radioactive decay"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">radioactive</span></a> <a href=""#alpha_decay""><span title=""See entry on this page at § alpha decay"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">alpha decay</span></a>, but may also be produced in other ways and given the same name.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alternating_current"" title=""Alternating current"">alternating current</a> (AC) (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A form of <a href=""#electric_current""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electric current"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electric current</span></a> in which the movement of <a href=""#electric_charge""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electric charge"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electric charge</span></a> periodically reverses direction. Contrast <i><a href=""#direct_current""><span title=""See entry on this page at § direct current"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">direct current</span></a></i>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ammeter"" title=""Ammeter"">ammeter</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">An instrument that is used to measure <a href=""#electric_current""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electric current"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electric current</span></a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amorphous_solid"" title=""Amorphous solid"">amorphous solid</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A type of solid which does not have a definite geometrical shape. Or its non-crystalline solid.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ampere"" title=""Ampere"">ampere</a> (A) (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Often abbreviated as <b>amp</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">The <a href=""#sI""><span title=""See entry on this page at § SI"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">SI</span></a> base unit of <a href=""#electric_current""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electric current"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electric current</span></a>, defined as one <a href=""#coulomb""><span title=""See entry on this page at § coulomb"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">coulomb</span></a> of <a href=""#electric_charge""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electric charge"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electric charge</span></a> per second.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amplifier"" title=""Amplifier"">amplifier</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">An <b>amplifier</b>, <b>electronic amplifier</b> or (informally) <b>amp</b> is an electronic device that can increase the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Power_(physics)"" title=""Power (physics)"">power</a> of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Signal_(information_theory)"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Signal (information theory)"">signal</a> (a time-varying <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Voltage"" title=""Voltage"">voltage</a> or <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electric_current"" title=""Electric current"">current</a>). It is a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Two-port_network"" title=""Two-port network"">two-port</a> electronic circuit that uses electric power from a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Power_supply"" title=""Power supply"">power supply</a> to increase the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amplitude"" title=""Amplitude"">amplitude</a> of a signal applied to its input terminals, producing a proportionally greater amplitude signal at its output.  The amount of amplification provided by an amplifier is measured by its <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gain_(electronics)"" title=""Gain (electronics)"">gain</a>: the ratio of output voltage, current, or power to input.  An amplifier is a circuit that has a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Power_gain"" title=""Power gain"">power gain</a> greater than one.<sup id=""cite_ref-Crecraft_9-0"" class=""reference""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#cite_note-Crecraft-9"">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-Agarwal_10-0"" class=""reference""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#cite_note-Agarwal-10"">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-Glisson_11-0"" class=""reference""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#cite_note-Glisson-11"">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup></dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amplitude"" title=""Amplitude"">amplitude</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The height of a <a href=""#wave""><span title=""See entry on this page at § wave"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">wave</span></a> measured from its center (normal) position.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angle_of_incidence_(optics)"" title=""Angle of incidence (optics)"">angle of incidence</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Geometric_optics"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Geometric optics"">geometric optics</a>, the <b>angle of incidence</b> is the angle between a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Incident_ray"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Incident ray"">ray incident</a> on a surface and the line perpendicular to the surface at the point of incidence, called the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surface_normal"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Surface normal"">normal</a>.  The ray can be formed by any wave: <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Light_wave"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Light wave"">optical</a>, <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sound_wave"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Sound wave"">acoustic</a>, <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Microwave"" title=""Microwave"">microwave</a>, <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/X-ray"" title=""X-ray"">X-ray</a>, etc.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angle_of_reflection"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Angle of reflection"">angle of reflection</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Reflection is the change in direction of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wavefront"" title=""Wavefront"">wavefront</a> at an <a href=""https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/interface"" class=""extiw"" title=""wikt:interface"">interface</a> between two different <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Medium_(optics)"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Medium (optics)"">media</a> so that the wavefront returns into the medium from which it originated. Common examples include the reflection of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Light"" title=""Light"">light</a>, <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sound"" title=""Sound"">sound</a> and <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Water_wave"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Water wave"">water waves</a>. The <i>law of reflection</i> says that for <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Specular_reflection"" title=""Specular reflection"">specular reflection</a> the angle at which the wave is incident on the surface equals the angle at which it is reflected. <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mirror"" title=""Mirror"">Mirrors</a> exhibit specular reflection.</dd>
"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%C3%85ngstr%C3%B6m"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Ångström"">ångström</a> (Å) (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A unit of length primarily used to measure <a href=""#subatomic_particle""><span title=""See entry on this page at § subatomic particle"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">subatomic particles</span></a> that is equal to 10<sup>−10</sup> meters (one ten-billionth of a meter) or 0.1 nanometers.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angular_acceleration"" title=""Angular acceleration"">angular acceleration</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Is the time rate of change of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angular_velocity"" title=""Angular velocity"">angular velocity</a>. In three dimensions, it is a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pseudovector"" title=""Pseudovector"">pseudovector</a>. In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SI"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""SI"">SI</a> units, it is measured in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radians"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Radians"">radians</a> per <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second"" title=""Second"">second</a> squared (rad/s<sup>2</sup>), and is usually denoted by the Greek letter <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alpha_(letter)"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Alpha (letter)"">alpha</a> (<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%CE%91"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Α"">α</a>).<sup id=""cite_ref-12"" class=""reference""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#cite_note-12"">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>. Just like angular velocity, there are two types of angular acceleration: spin angular acceleration and orbital angular acceleration, representing the time rate of change of spin angular velocity and orbital angular velocity respectively. Unlike linear acceleration, angular acceleration need not be caused by a net external <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Torque"" title=""Torque"">torque</a>. For example, a figure skater can speed up her rotation (thereby obtaining an angular acceleration) simply by contracting her arms inwards, which involves no <i>external</i> torque.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angular_displacement"" title=""Angular displacement"">angular displacement</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Angular displacement of a body is the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angle"" title=""Angle"">angle</a> in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radian"" title=""Radian"">radians</a> (<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Degree_(angle)"" title=""Degree (angle)"">degrees</a>, <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turn_(geometry)"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Turn (geometry)"">revolutions</a>) through which a point revolves around a centre or line has been rotated in a specified sense about a specified <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rotation"" title=""Rotation"">axis</a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angular_frequency"" title=""Angular frequency"">angular frequency</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Angular frequency <i>ω</i> (also referred to by the terms angular speed, radial frequency, circular frequency, orbital frequency, radian frequency, and pulsatance) is a scalar measure of rotation rate. It refers to the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angular_displacement"" title=""Angular displacement"">angular displacement</a> per unit time (e.g., in rotation) or the rate of change of the phase of a sinusoidal waveform (e.g., in oscillations and waves), or as the rate of change of the argument of the sine function.

Angular frequency (or angular speed) is the magnitude of the vector quantity <i><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angular_velocity"" title=""Angular velocity"">angular velocity</a></i>. The term <b>angular frequency vector</b> <span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle {\vec {\omega }}}"">

One <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turn_(geometry)"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Turn (geometry)"">revolution</a> is equal to 2π <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radian"" title=""Radian"">radians</a>, hence<sup id=""cite_ref-UP1_13-1"" class=""reference""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#cite_note-UP1-13"">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-14"" class=""reference""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#cite_note-14"">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>

<dl><dd><span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle \omega ={{2\pi } \over T}={2\pi f},}"">

where:
<dl><dd><i>ω</i> is the angular frequency or angular speed (measured in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radians_per_second"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Radians per second"">radians per second</a>),</dd>
<dd><i>T</i> is the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frequency"" title=""Frequency"">period</a> (measured in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second"" title=""Second"">seconds</a>),</dd>
<dd><i>f</i> is the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ordinary_frequency"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Ordinary frequency"">ordinary frequency</a> (measured in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hertz"" title=""Hertz"">hertz</a>) (sometimes symbolised with <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nu_(letter)"" title=""Nu (letter)""><i>ν</i></a>).</dd></dl></dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angular_momentum"" title=""Angular momentum"">angular momentum</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">(Rarely, moment of momentum or rotational momentum) is the rotational equivalent of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linear_momentum"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Linear momentum"">linear momentum</a>. It is an important quantity in physics because it is a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conservation_law"" title=""Conservation law"">conserved quantity</a>—the total angular momentum of a closed system remains constant.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angular_velocity"" title=""Angular velocity"">angular velocity</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Refers to how fast an object rotates or revolves relative to another point, i.e. how fast the angular position or orientation of an object changes with time. There are two types of angular velocity: orbital angular velocity and spin angular velocity. Spin angular velocity refers to how fast a rigid body rotates with respect to its centre of rotation. Orbital angular velocity refers to how fast a rigid body's centre of rotation revolves about a fixed origin, i.e. the time rate of change of its angular position relative to the origin. In general, angular velocity is measured in angle per unit time, e.g. <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radians_per_second"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Radians per second"">radians per second</a>. The  <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SI"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""SI"">SI</a> unit of angular velocity is expressed as radians/sec with the radian having a dimensionless value of unity, thus the SI units of angular velocity are listed as <i>1/sec</i>. Angular velocity is usually represented by the symbol <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Omega"" title=""Omega"">omega</a> (ω, sometimes Ω). By convention, positive angular velocity indicates counter-clockwise rotation, while negative is clockwise.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anion"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Anion"">anion</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A negatively charged <a href=""#ion""><span title=""See entry on this page at § ion"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">ion</span></a>. Contrast <i><a href=""#cation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § cation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">cation</span></a></i>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Annihilation"" title=""Annihilation"">annihilation</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Particle_physics"" title=""Particle physics"">particle physics</a>, annihilation is the process that occurs when a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subatomic_particle"" title=""Subatomic particle"">subatomic particle</a> collides with its respective <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antiparticle"" title=""Antiparticle"">antiparticle</a> to produce other particles, such as an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electron"" title=""Electron"">electron</a> colliding with a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Positron"" title=""Positron"">positron</a> to produce two <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Photon"" title=""Photon"">photons</a>.<sup id=""cite_ref-15"" class=""reference""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#cite_note-15"">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The total <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Energy"" title=""Energy"">energy</a> and <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Momentum"" title=""Momentum"">momentum</a> of the initial pair are conserved in the process and distributed among a set of other particles in the final state. Antiparticles have exactly opposite additive <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_number"" title=""Quantum number"">quantum numbers</a> from particles, so the sums of all quantum numbers of such an original pair are zero. Hence, any set of particles may be produced whose total quantum numbers are also zero as long as <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conservation_of_energy"" title=""Conservation of energy"">conservation of energy</a> and <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conservation_of_momentum"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Conservation of momentum"">conservation of momentum</a> are obeyed.<sup id=""cite_ref-Particleadventure.org_16-0"" class=""reference""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#cite_note-Particleadventure.org-16"">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup></dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anode"" title=""Anode"">anode</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The electrode through which a conventional <a href=""#current""><span title=""See entry on this page at § current"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electric current</span></a> flows into a polarized electrical device; the direction of current flow is, by convention, opposite to the direction of <a href=""#electron""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electron"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electron</span></a> flow, and so electrons flow <i>out of</i> the anode. In a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galvanic_cell"" title=""Galvanic cell"">galvanic cell</a>, the anode is the negative terminal or pole which emits electrons toward the external part of an <a href=""#electrical_circuit""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electrical circuit"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electrical circuit</span></a>. However, in an <a href=""#electrolytic_cell""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electrolytic cell"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electrolytic cell</span></a>, the anode is the wire or plate having excess <i>positive</i> charge, so named because negatively charged <a href=""#anion""><span title=""See entry on this page at § anion"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">anions</span></a> tend to move towards it. Contrast <i><a href=""#cathode""><span title=""See entry on this page at § cathode"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">cathode</span></a></i>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anti-gravity"" title=""Anti-gravity"">anti-gravity</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">(Also known as <i>non-gravitational field</i>) is a theory of creating a place or object that is free from the force of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravity"" title=""Gravity"">gravity</a>. It does not refer to the lack of weight under gravity experienced in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Free_fall"" title=""Free fall"">free fall</a> or <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orbit"" title=""Orbit"">orbit</a>, or to balancing the force of gravity with some other force, such as electromagnetism or aerodynamic lift.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antimatter"" title=""Antimatter"">antimatter</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p>Looks like this term doesn't have a definition yet.</p>

"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antineutron"" title=""Antineutron"">antineutron</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Is the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antiparticle"" title=""Antiparticle"">antiparticle</a> of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neutron"" title=""Neutron"">neutron</a> with symbol <span style=""white-space:nowrap;""><span style=""display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:right""><sup style=""font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline""></sup><br /><sub style=""font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline""></sub></span><span style=""text-decoration:overline;"">n</span><span style=""display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:0.8em;line-height:1.0em;font-size:80%;text-align:left""><sup style=""font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline""></sup><br /><sub style=""font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline""></sub></span></span>. It differs from the neutron only in that some of its properties have <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Additive_inverse"" title=""Additive inverse"">equal magnitude but opposite sign</a>. It has the same <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mass"" title=""Mass"">mass</a> as the neutron, and no net <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electric_charge"" title=""Electric charge"">electric charge</a>, but has opposite <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baryon_number"" title=""Baryon number"">baryon number</a> (+1 for neutron, −1 for the antineutron). This is because the antineutron is composed of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antiquark"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Antiquark"">antiquarks</a>, while neutrons are composed of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quark"" title=""Quark"">quarks</a>. The antineutron consists of one <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Up_antiquark"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Up antiquark"">up antiquark</a> and two <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Down_antiquark"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Down antiquark"">down antiquarks</a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antiparticle"" title=""Antiparticle"">antiparticle</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Particle_physics"" title=""Particle physics"">particle physics</a>, every type of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Particle"" title=""Particle"">particle</a> has an associated antiparticle with the same <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mass"" title=""Mass"">mass</a> but with opposite <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charge_(physics)"" title=""Charge (physics)"">physical charges</a> (such as <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electric_charge"" title=""Electric charge"">electric charge</a>). For example, the antiparticle of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electron"" title=""Electron"">electron</a> is the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antielectron"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Antielectron"">antielectron</a> (which is often referred to as <i>positron</i>). While the electron has a negative electric charge, the positron has a positive electric charge, and is produced naturally in certain types of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radioactive_decay"" title=""Radioactive decay"">radioactive decay</a>.  The opposite is also true: the antiparticle of the positron is the electron.  Some particles, such as the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Photon"" title=""Photon"">photon</a>, are their own antiparticle. Otherwise, for each pair of antiparticle partners, one is designated as normal matter (the kind all matter usually interacted with is made of), and the other (usually given the prefix ""anti-"") as <i><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antimatter"" title=""Antimatter"">antimatter</a></i>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antiproton"" title=""Antiproton"">antiproton</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p>Looks like this term doesn't have a definition yet.</p>

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antiquark"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Antiquark"">antiquark</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">For every quark flavor there is a corresponding type of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Antiparticle"" title=""Antiparticle"">antiparticle</a>, known as an antiquark, that differs from the quark only in that some of its properties (such as the electric charge) have <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Additive_inverse"" title=""Additive inverse"">equal magnitude but opposite sign</a>.</dd>"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archimedes%27_principle"" title=""Archimedes&#39; principle"">Archimedes' principle</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">States that the upward <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Buoyancy"" title=""Buoyancy"">buoyant force</a> that is exerted on a body immersed in a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fluid"" title=""Fluid"">fluid</a>, whether fully or partially submerged, is equal to the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Weight"" title=""Weight"">weight</a> of the fluid that the body <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Displacement_(fluid)"" title=""Displacement (fluid)"">displaces</a> and acts in the upward direction at the center of mass of the displaced fluid.<sup id=""cite_ref-khanacademy.org_17-0"" class=""reference""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#cite_note-khanacademy.org-17"">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup></dd>"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomical_unit"" title=""Astronomical unit"">astronomical unit</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">(Symbol: <b>au</b>,<sup id=""cite_ref-IAUresB2_18-0"" class=""reference""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#cite_note-IAUresB2-18"">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-mnras_style_19-0"" class=""reference""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#cite_note-mnras_style-19"">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-20"" class=""reference""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#cite_note-20"">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> <b>ua</b>,<sup id=""cite_ref-21"" class=""reference""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#cite_note-21"">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> or <b>AU</b>) is a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unit_of_length"" title=""Unit of length"">unit of length</a>, roughly the distance from <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Earth"" title=""Earth"">Earth</a> to the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sun"" title=""Sun"">Sun</a>. However, that distance varies as Earth orbits the Sun, from a maximum (<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aphelion"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Aphelion"">aphelion</a>) to a minimum (<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perihelion"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Perihelion"">perihelion</a>) and back again once a year. Originally conceived as the average of Earth's aphelion and perihelion, since 2012 it has been defined as exactly 149,597,870,700 metres, or about 150&#160;million kilometres (93&#160;million miles).<sup id=""cite_ref-22"" class=""reference""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#cite_note-22"">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> The astronomical unit is used primarily for measuring distances within the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solar_System"" title=""Solar System"">Solar System</a> or around other stars. It is also a fundamental component in the definition of another unit of astronomical length, the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parsec"" title=""Parsec"">parsec</a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astrophysics"" title=""Astrophysics"">astrophysics</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The branch of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Astronomy"" title=""Astronomy"">astronomy</a> that deals with the physics of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Universe"" title=""Universe"">Universe</a>, especially with the nature of celestial bodies rather than their positions or motions in space.</dd>"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Avogadro_constant"" title=""Avogadro constant"">Avogadro constant</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">(6.02214086 × 1023 mol<sup>−1</sup>) In chemistry and physics, the Avogadro constant (named after the scientist Amedeo Avogadro) is the number of constituent particles, usually atoms or molecules, that are contained in the amount of substance given by one mole.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Avogadro%27s_law"" title=""Avogadro&#39;s law"">Avogadro's law</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A physical law which states that volumes of gases which are equal to each other at the same temperature and pressure will contain equal numbers of molecules.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Avogadro%27s_number"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Avogadro&#39;s number"">Avogadro's number</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The number of molecules in exactly 12g of carbon-12, equaling 6.022 x 10<sup>23</sup>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Axion"" title=""Axion"">axion</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A hypothetical <a href=""#subatomic_particle""><span title=""See entry on this page at § subatomic particle"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">subatomic particle</span></a> postulated to account for the rarity of processes that break charge-parity symmetry. It is very light, electrically neutral, and pseudoscalar.</dd>"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Babinet%27s_principle"" title=""Babinet&#39;s principle"">Babinet's principle</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A theorem concerning diffraction which states that the diffraction pattern from an opaque body is identical to that from a hole of the same size and shape except for the overall forward beam intensity.</dd>"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Balmer_series"" title=""Balmer series"">Balmer series</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Or Balmer lines, in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atomic_physics"" title=""Atomic physics"">atomic physics</a>, is one of a set of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogen_spectral_series"" title=""Hydrogen spectral series"">six named series</a> describing the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spectral_line"" title=""Spectral line"">spectral line</a> emissions of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hydrogen_atom"" title=""Hydrogen atom"">hydrogen atom</a>. The Balmer series is calculated using the Balmer formula, an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Empirical"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Empirical"">empirical</a> equation discovered by <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johann_Balmer"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Johann Balmer"">Johann Balmer</a> in 1885.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barometer"" title=""Barometer"">barometer</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A scientific instrument used in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meteorology"" title=""Meteorology"">meteorology</a> to measure <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atmospheric_pressure"" title=""Atmospheric pressure"">atmospheric pressure</a>. Pressure tendency can forecast short-term changes in the weather.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Baryon"" title=""Baryon"">baryon</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary""> A <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Subatomic_particle"" title=""Subatomic particle"">subatomic particle</a> such as a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proton"" title=""Proton"">proton</a> or a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neutron"" title=""Neutron"">neutron</a>, made of (usually) three <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quarks"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Quarks"">quarks</a>. Nearly all <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Matter"" title=""Matter"">matter</a> you are likely to encounter is baryonic matter.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battery_(electricity)"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Battery (electricity)"">battery</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A combination of two or more electrical cells which produces <a href=""#electricity""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electricity"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electricity</span></a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Beam_(structure)"" title=""Beam (structure)"">beam</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A structural element that is capable of withstanding load primarily by resisting bending.Beams are traditionally descriptions of building or civil engineering structural elements, but smaller structures such as truck or automobile frames, machine frames, and other mechanical or structural systems contain beam structures that are designed and analyzed in a similar fashion.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bending"" title=""Bending"">bending</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also known as <b>flexure</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">The behavior of a slender structural element subjected to an external load applied perpendicularly to a longitudinal axis of the element.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bending_moment"" title=""Bending moment"">bending moment</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Is the reaction induced in a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Structural_element"" title=""Structural element"">structural element</a> when an external <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Force"" title=""Force"">force</a> or <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moment_of_force"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Moment of force"">moment</a> is applied to the element causing the element to <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bending"" title=""Bending"">bend</a>.<sup id=""cite_ref-23"" class=""reference""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#cite_note-23"">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup id=""cite_ref-24"" class=""reference""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#cite_note-24"">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> The most common or simplest structural element subjected to bending moments is the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Beam_(structure)"" title=""Beam (structure)"">beam</a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bernoulli%27s_equation"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Bernoulli&#39;s equation"">Bernoulli equation</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p>Looks like this term doesn't have a definition yet.</p>

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bernoulli%27s_principle"" title=""Bernoulli&#39;s principle"">Bernoulli's principle</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fluid_dynamics"" title=""Fluid dynamics"">fluid dynamics</a>, Bernoulli's principle states that an increase in the speed of a fluid occurs simultaneously with a decrease in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pressure"" title=""Pressure"">pressure</a> or a decrease in the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fluid"" title=""Fluid"">fluid</a>'s <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Potential_energy"" title=""Potential energy"">potential energy</a>.<sup id=""cite_ref-Clancy1975_25-0"" class=""reference""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#cite_note-Clancy1975-25"">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup class=""reference"" style=""white-space:nowrap;"">(<span>Ch.3</span>)</sup><sup id=""cite_ref-Batchelor2000_26-0"" class=""reference""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#cite_note-Batchelor2000-26"">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup><sup class=""reference"" style=""white-space:nowrap;"">(<span>§ 3.5</span>)</sup></dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bessel_function"" title=""Bessel function"">Bessel function</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Bessel functions, first defined by the mathematician <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Daniel_Bernoulli"" title=""Daniel Bernoulli"">Daniel Bernoulli</a> and then generalized by <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Friedrich_Bessel"" title=""Friedrich Bessel"">Friedrich Bessel</a>, are canonical solutions <span class=""texhtml""><i>y</i>(<i>x</i>)</span> of Bessel's <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Differential_equation"" title=""Differential equation"">differential equation</a>

<dl><dd><span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle x^{2}{\frac {d^{2}y}{dx^{2}}}+x{\frac {dy}{dx}}+\left(x^{2}-\alpha ^{2}\right)y=0}"">

  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f37ea24b1f82f74dbc1f8cf1348020726772f891"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:32.912ex; height:6.009ex;"" alt=""{\displaystyle x^{2}{\frac {d^{2}y}{dx^{2}}}+x{\frac {dy}{dx}}+\left(x^{2}-\alpha ^{2}\right)y=0}""/></span></dd></dl>

for an arbitrary <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complex_number"" title=""Complex number"">complex number</a> <span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">α</span>, the <i>order</i> of the Bessel function. Although <span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">α</span> and <span class=""texhtml"">−<i>α</i></span> produce the same differential equation, it is conventional to define different Bessel functions for these two values in such a way that the Bessel functions are mostly smooth functions of <span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">α</span>.

The most important cases are when <span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">α</span> is an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integer"" title=""Integer"">integer</a> or <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Half-integer"" title=""Half-integer"">half-integer</a>. Bessel functions for integer <span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">α</span> are also known as cylinder functions or the <b><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cylindrical_harmonics"" title=""Cylindrical harmonics"">cylindrical harmonics</a></b> because they appear in the solution to <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Laplace%27s_equation"" title=""Laplace&#39;s equation"">Laplace's equation</a> in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cylindrical_coordinates"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Cylindrical coordinates"">cylindrical coordinates</a>. <b><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spherical_Bessel_functions"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Spherical Bessel functions"">Spherical Bessel functions</a></b> with half-integer <span class=""texhtml mvar"" style=""font-style:italic;"">α</span> are obtained when the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Helmholtz_equation"" title=""Helmholtz equation"">Helmholtz equation</a> is solved in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Spherical_coordinates"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Spherical coordinates"">spherical coordinates</a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Beta_decay"" title=""Beta decay"">beta decay</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">In <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_physics"" title=""Nuclear physics"">nuclear physics</a>, beta decay (<i>β</i>-decay) is a type of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radioactive_decay"" title=""Radioactive decay"">radioactive decay</a> in which a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Beta_particle"" title=""Beta particle"">beta particle</a> (fast energetic <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electron"" title=""Electron"">electron</a> or <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Positron"" title=""Positron"">positron</a>) is emitted from an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atomic_nucleus"" title=""Atomic nucleus"">atomic nucleus</a>, transforming the original <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclide"" title=""Nuclide"">nuclide</a> to its <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Isobar_(nuclide)"" title=""Isobar (nuclide)"">isobar</a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Beta_particle"" title=""Beta particle"">beta particle</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A high-energy, high-speed <a href=""#electron""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electron"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electron</span></a> or <a href=""#positron""><span title=""See entry on this page at § positron"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">positron</span></a> emitted by certain types of radioactive atomic nuclei.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_Bang"" title=""Big Bang"">Big Bang</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The prevailing cosmological model that describes the early development of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Universe"" title=""Universe"">Universe</a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Binding_energy"" title=""Binding energy"">binding energy</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The mechanical energy required to disassemble a whole into separate parts. A bound system typically has a lower potential energy than the sum of its constituent parts.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Binomial_random_variable"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Binomial random variable"">binomial random variable</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biophysics"" title=""Biophysics"">biophysics</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">An interdisciplinary science using methods of and theories from physics to study <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biology"" title=""Biology"">biological</a> systems.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Black_body"" title=""Black body"">black body</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A hypothetical idealized physical body that completely absorbs all incident <a href=""#electromagnetic_radiation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electromagnetic radiation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electromagnetic radiation</span></a>, regardless of <a href=""#frequency""><span title=""See entry on this page at § frequency"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">frequency</span></a> or <a href=""#angle_of_incidence""><span title=""See entry on this page at § angle of incidence"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">angle of incidence</span></a>. Perfect black bodies are imagined as substitutes for actual physical bodies in many theoretical discussions of <a href=""#thermodynamics""><span title=""See entry on this page at § thermodynamics"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">thermodynamics</span></a>, and the construction of nearly perfect black bodies in the real world remains a topic of interest for materials engineers. Contrast <i><a href=""#white_body""><span title=""See entry on this page at § white body"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">white body</span></a></i>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Black-body_radiation"" title=""Black-body radiation"">black-body radiation</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The type of electromagnetic radiation within or surrounding a body in thermodynamic equilibrium with its environment, or emitted by a <a href=""#black_body""><span title=""See entry on this page at § black body"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">black body</span></a> (an opaque and non-reflective body) held at constant, uniform temperature. The radiation has a specific spectrum and intensity that depends only on the temperature of the body.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Block_and_tackle"" title=""Block and tackle"">block and tackle</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A system of two or more <a href=""#pulley""><span title=""See entry on this page at § pulley"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">pulleys</span></a> with a rope or cable threaded between them, usually used to lift or pull heavy loads.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bohr_model"" title=""Bohr model"">Bohr model</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"

"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boiling_point"" title=""Boiling point"">boiling point</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The <a href=""#temperature""><span title=""See entry on this page at § temperature"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">temperature</span></a> at which a <a href=""#liquid""><span title=""See entry on this page at § liquid"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">liquid</span></a> undergoes a phase change into a <a href=""#gas""><span title=""See entry on this page at § gas"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">gas</span></a>; the vapour pressure of liquid and gas are equal at this temperature.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boiling_point_elevation"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Boiling point elevation"">boiling point elevation</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Describes the phenomenon that the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boiling_point"" title=""Boiling point"">boiling point</a> of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liquid"" title=""Liquid"">liquid</a> (a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solvent"" title=""Solvent"">solvent</a>) will be higher when another compound is added, meaning that a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solution"" title=""Solution"">solution</a> has a higher boiling point than a pure solvent. This happens whenever a non-volatile solute, such as a salt, is added to a pure solvent, such as water. The boiling point can be measured accurately using an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ebullioscope"" title=""Ebullioscope"">ebullioscope</a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boltzmann_constant"" title=""Boltzmann constant"">Boltzmann constant</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A physical constant relating the average <a href=""#kinetic_energy""><span title=""See entry on this page at § kinetic energy"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">kinetic energy</span></a> of the particles in a <a href=""#gas""><span title=""See entry on this page at § gas"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">gas</span></a> with the <a href=""#temperature""><span title=""See entry on this page at § temperature"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">temperature</span></a> of the gas. It is the gas constant R divided by the Avogadro constant NA.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bose%E2%80%93Einstein_condensate"" title=""Bose–Einstein condensate"">Bose–Einstein condensate</a> (BEC) (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boson"" title=""Boson"">boson</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A type of <a href=""#particle""><span title=""See entry on this page at § particle"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">particle</span></a> that behaves according to <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bose%E2%80%93Einstein_statistics"" title=""Bose–Einstein statistics"">Bose–Einstein statistics</a> and possesses integer <a href=""#spin""><span title=""See entry on this page at § spin"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">spin</span></a>. Bosons include <a href=""#elementary_particle""><span title=""See entry on this page at § elementary particle"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">elementary particles</span></a> such as <a href=""#photon""><span title=""See entry on this page at § photon"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">photons</span></a>, <a href=""#gluon""><span title=""See entry on this page at § gluon"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">gluons</span></a>, <a href=""#w_and_Z_bosons""><span title=""See entry on this page at § W and Z bosons"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">W and Z bosons</span></a>, <a href=""#higgs_boson""><span title=""See entry on this page at § Higgs boson"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">Higgs bosons</span></a>, and the hypothetical <a href=""#graviton""><span title=""See entry on this page at § graviton"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">graviton</span></a>, as well as certain <a href=""#composite_particle""><span title=""See entry on this page at § composite particle"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">composite particles</span></a> such as <a href=""#meson""><span title=""See entry on this page at § meson"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">mesons</span></a> and <a href=""#stable_nuclide""><span title=""See entry on this page at § stable nuclide"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">stable nuclides</span></a> of even <a href=""#mass_number""><span title=""See entry on this page at § mass number"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">mass number</span></a>. Bosons constitute one of two main classes of particles, the other being <a href=""#fermion""><span title=""See entry on this page at § fermion"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">fermions</span></a>. Unlike fermions, there is no limit to the number of bosons that can occupy the same <a href=""#quantum_state""><span title=""See entry on this page at § quantum state"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">quantum state</span></a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boyle%27s_law"" title=""Boyle&#39;s law"">Boyle's law</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The volume of a given mass of a gas at constant temperature is inversely proportional to the pressure.</dd>"
