"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_thermal_unit"" title=""British thermal unit"">british thermal unit</a> (btu) (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">An Imperial unit of <a href=""#energy""><span title=""See entry on this page at § energy"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">energy</span></a> defined as the amount of energy needed to heat one pound of water by one degree Fahrenheit; 1 btu is equal to about 1055 <a href=""#joule""><span title=""See entry on this page at § joule"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">joules</span></a>. In scientific contexts the btu has largely been replaced by the SI unit of energy, the joule.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brittleness"" title=""Brittleness"">brittleness</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The tendency of a material to break without significant <a href=""#plasticity""><span title=""See entry on this page at § plasticity"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">plastic</span></a> <a href=""#deformation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § deformation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">deformation</span></a> when subjected to <a href=""#stress""><span title=""See entry on this page at § stress"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">stress</span></a>. Brittle materials absorb relatively little energy prior to fracture, even those of high strength. Breaking is often accompanied by a snapping sound.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brownian_motion"" title=""Brownian motion"">Brownian motion</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called <b>pedesis</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">The presumably random movement of particles suspended in a fluid (liquid or gas) resulting from their bombardment by the fast-moving atoms or molecules in the gas or liquid.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bulk_modulus"" title=""Bulk modulus"">Bulk modulus</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A measure of a substance's resistance to uniform compression defined as the ratio of the infinitesimal pressure increase to the resulting relative decrease of the volume. Its base unit is the <a href=""#pascal""><span title=""See entry on this page at § pascal"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">pascal</span></a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Buoyancy"" title=""Buoyancy"">buoyancy</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">An upward <a href=""#force""><span title=""See entry on this page at § force"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">force</span></a> exerted by a fluid that opposes the weight of an immersed object.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Calculus"" title=""Calculus"">calculus</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A branch of <a href=""#mathematics""><span title=""See entry on this page at § mathematics"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">mathematics</span></a> that studies change and has two major sub-fields: <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Differential_calculus"" title=""Differential calculus"">differential calculus</a> (concerning rates of change and slopes of curves), and <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Integral_calculus"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Integral calculus"">integral calculus</a> (concerning accumulation of quantities and the areas under and between curves). These two branches are related to each other by the fundamental theorem of calculus.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Capacitance"" title=""Capacitance"">capacitance</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Is the ratio of the change in an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electric_charge"" title=""Electric charge"">electric charge</a> in a system to the corresponding change in its <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electric_potential"" title=""Electric potential"">electric potential</a>. There are two closely related notions of capacitance: <i>self capacitance</i> and <i>mutual capacitance</i>. Any object that can be electrically charged exhibits <i>self capacitance</i>. A material with a large self capacitance holds more electric charge at a given <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Voltage"" title=""Voltage"">voltage</a> than one with low capacitance. The notion of <i>mutual capacitance</i> is particularly important for understanding the operations of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Capacitor"" title=""Capacitor"">capacitor</a>, one of the three elementary <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nonlinear_circuit"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Nonlinear circuit"">linear</a> electronic components (along with <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Resistors"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Resistors"">resistors</a> and <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inductors"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Inductors"">inductors</a>).</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Capacitive_reactance"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Capacitive reactance"">capacitive reactance</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A capacitor consists of two <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electric_conduction"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Electric conduction"">conductors</a> separated by an <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electrical_insulation"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Electrical insulation"">insulator</a>, also known as a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dielectric"" title=""Dielectric"">dielectric</a>.
<i>Capacitive reactance</i> is an opposition to the change of voltage across an element. Capacitive reactance <span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle \scriptstyle {X_{C}}}"">

</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/59240151f860510756e15891b331c0bc0ab20594"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.249ex; height:1.676ex;"" alt=""\scriptstyle {C}""/></span>.<sup id=""cite_ref-Irwin_27-0"" class=""reference""><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glossary_of_physics#cite_note-Irwin-27"">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup></dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carnot_cycle"" title=""Carnot cycle"">Carnot cycle</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Is a theoretical ideal <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermodynamic_cycle"" title=""Thermodynamic cycle"">thermodynamic cycle</a> proposed by French physicist <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nicolas_L%C3%A9onard_Sadi_Carnot"" title=""Nicolas Léonard Sadi Carnot"">Sadi Carnot</a> in 1824 and expanded upon by others in the 1830s and 1840s. It provides an upper limit on the efficiency that any classical thermodynamic engine can achieve during the conversion of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heat"" title=""Heat"">heat</a> into <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Work_(physics)"" title=""Work (physics)"">work</a>, or conversely, the efficiency of a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Refrigeration"" title=""Refrigeration"">refrigeration</a> system in creating a temperature difference by the application of work to the system. It is not an actual thermodynamic cycle but is a theoretical construct.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cartesian_coordinate_system"" title=""Cartesian coordinate system"">Cartesian coordinate system</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Is a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coordinate_system"" title=""Coordinate system"">coordinate system</a> that specifies each <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Point_(geometry)"" title=""Point (geometry)"">point</a> uniquely in a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plane_(geometry)"" title=""Plane (geometry)"">plane</a> by a set of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Number"" title=""Number"">numerical</a> coordinates, which are the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Positive_and_negative_numbers"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Positive and negative numbers"">signed</a> distances to the point from two fixed <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perpendicular"" title=""Perpendicular"">perpendicular</a> oriented lines, measured in the same <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Unit_length"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Unit length"">unit of length</a>. Each reference line is called a <i>coordinate axis</i> or just <i>axis</i> (plural <i>axes</i>) of the system, and the point where they meet is its <i><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Origin_(mathematics)"" title=""Origin (mathematics)"">origin</a></i>, at ordered pair <span class=""nowrap"">(0, 0)</span>. The coordinates can also be defined as the positions of the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orthogonal_projection"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Orthogonal projection"">perpendicular projections</a> of the point onto the two axes, expressed as signed distances from the origin.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cathode"" title=""Cathode"">cathode</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The electrode through which a conventional <a href=""#current""><span title=""See entry on this page at § current"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electric current</span></a> flows out of a polarized electrical device; the direction of current flow is, by convention, opposite to the direction of <a href=""#electron""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electron"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electron</span></a> flow, and so electrons flow <i>into</i> the cathode. In a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galvanic_cell"" title=""Galvanic cell"">galvanic cell</a>, the cathode is the positive terminal or pole which accepts electrons flowing from the external part of an <a href=""#electrical_circuit""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electrical circuit"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electrical circuit</span></a>. However, in an <a href=""#electrolytic_cell""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electrolytic cell"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electrolytic cell</span></a>, the cathode is the wire or plate having excess <i>negative</i> charge, so named because positively charged <a href=""#cation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § cation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">cations</span></a> tend to move towards it. Contrast <i><a href=""#anode""><span title=""See entry on this page at § anode"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">anode</span></a></i>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cathode_ray"" title=""Cathode ray"">cathode ray</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p>Looks like this term doesn't have a definition yet.</p>

<dd class=""glossary"">A positively charged <a href=""#ion""><span title=""See entry on this page at § ion"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">ion</span></a>. <dd class=""glossary"">A scale and unit of measurement of <a href=""#temperature""><span title=""See entry on this page at § temperature"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">temperature</span></a>, also known as Centigrade.</dd>"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Center_of_gravity"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Center of gravity"">center of gravity</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The point in a body around which the resultant torque due to gravity forces vanish. Near the surface of the earth, where the gravity acts downward as a parallel force field, the center of gravity and the center of mass are the same.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Center_of_mass"" title=""Center of mass"">center of mass</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A distribution of mass in space is the unique point where the weighted relative position of the distributed mass sums to zero.</dd>"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Centrifugal_force"" title=""Centrifugal force"">centrifugal force</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The apparent outward force that draws a rotating body away from the centre of rotation. It is caused by the inertia of the body as the body's path is continually redirected.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Centripetal_force"" title=""Centripetal force"">centripetal force</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A force which keeps a body moving with a uniform speed along a circular path and is directed along the radius towards the centre.</dd>"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_mechanics"" title=""Classical mechanics"">classical mechanics</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called <b>Newtonian mechanics</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">A sub-field of <a href=""#mechanics""><span title=""See entry on this page at § mechanics"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">mechanics</span></a> concerned with the set of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physical_laws"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Physical laws"">physical laws</a> describing the motion of bodies under the collective actions of a system of <a href=""#force""><span title=""See entry on this page at § force"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">forces</span></a>.</dd>"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Displacement_(fluid)"" title=""Displacement (fluid)"">displacement (fluid)</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Occurs when an object is immersed in a fluid, pushing it out of the way and taking its place. The volume of the fluid displaced can then be measured, and from this the volume of the immersed object can be deduced (the volume of the immersed object will be exactly equal to the volume of the displaced fluid).</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Displacement_(vector)"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Displacement (vector)"">displacement (vector)</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The shortest distance from the initial to the final position of a point. Thus, it is the length of an imaginary straight path, typically distinct from the path actually travelled by.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Distance"" title=""Distance"">distance</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A numerical description of how far apart objects are.</dd>"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Doppler_effect"" title=""Doppler effect"">Doppler effect</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The change in frequency of a wave (or other periodic event) for an observer moving relative to its source.The received frequency is higher (compared to the emitted frequency) during the approach, it is identical at the instant of passing by, and it is lower during the recession.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Drag_(physics)"" title=""Drag (physics)"">drag</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Forces which act on a solid object in the direction of the relative fluid flow velocity. Unlike other resistive forces, such as dry friction, which is nearly independent of velocity, drag forces depend on velocity.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ductility"" title=""Ductility"">ductility</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A solid material's ability to deform under tensile stress; this is often characterized by the material's ability to be stretched into a wire.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dynamics_(mechanics)"" title=""Dynamics (mechanics)"">dynamics</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The branch of <a href=""#classical_mechanics""><span title=""See entry on this page at § classical mechanics"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">classical mechanics</span></a> that studies <a href=""#force""><span title=""See entry on this page at § force"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">forces</span></a> and <a href=""#torque""><span title=""See entry on this page at § torque"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">torques</span></a> and their effects on <a href=""#motion""><span title=""See entry on this page at § motion"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">motion</span></a>, as opposed to <a href=""#kinematics""><span title=""See entry on this page at § kinematics"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">kinematics</span></a>, which studies motion without reference to these forces.</dd>"

There are two types of electric charge: positive and negative.</dd>"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electromotive_force"" title=""Electromotive force"">electromotive force</a> (<span class=""mwe-math-element""><span class=""mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y"" style=""display: none;""><math xmlns=""http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML""  alttext=""{\displaystyle {\mathcal {E}}}"">
  <semantics>
    <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
      <mstyle displaystyle=""true"" scriptlevel=""0"">
        <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
          <mrow class=""MJX-TeXAtom-ORD"">
            <mi class=""MJX-tex-caligraphic"" mathvariant=""script"">E</mi>
          </mrow>
        </mrow>
      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding=""application/x-tex"">{\displaystyle {\mathcal {E}}}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src=""https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9c298ed828ff778065aeb5f0f305097f55bb9ae0"" class=""mwe-math-fallback-image-inline"" aria-hidden=""true"" style=""vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.311ex; height:2.176ex;"" alt=""{\mathcal {E}}""/></span>) (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also abbreviated <b>emf</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">The electrical intensity or ""pressure"" developed by a source of electrical energy such as a <a href=""#battery""><span title=""See entry on this page at § battery"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">battery</span></a> or <a href=""#electric_generator""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electric generator"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">generator</span></a> and measured in <a href=""#volt""><span title=""See entry on this page at § volt"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">volts</span></a>. Any device that converts other forms of <a href=""#energy""><span title=""See entry on this page at § energy"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">energy</span></a> into electrical energy provides electromotive force as its output.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electron"" title=""Electron"">electron</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A <a href=""#subatomic_particle""><span title=""See entry on this page at § subatomic particle"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">subatomic particle</span></a> with a negative <a href=""#elementary_charge""><span title=""See entry on this page at § elementary charge"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">elementary</span></a> <a href=""#electric_charge""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electric charge"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electric charge</span></a>.</dd>"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electron_paramagnetic_resonance"" title=""Electron paramagnetic resonance"">electron paramagnetic resonance</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called <b>electron spin resonance (ESR)</b> and <b>electron magnetic resonance (EMR)</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">A method for studying materials with unpaired <a href=""#electron""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electron"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electrons</span></a> which makes use of the <a href=""#zeeman_effect""><span title=""See entry on this page at § Zeeman effect"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">Zeeman effect</span></a>. It shares some basic principles with <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_magnetic_resonance"" title=""Nuclear magnetic resonance"">nuclear magnetic resonance</a> (NMR).</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electronvolt"" title=""Electronvolt"">electronvolt</a> (eV) (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A unit of <a href=""#energy""><span title=""See entry on this page at § energy"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">energy</span></a> equal to approximately 1.6×10<sup>−19</sup> <a href=""#joule""><span title=""See entry on this page at § joule"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">joule</span></a>. By definition, it is the amount of energy gained by the charge of a single <a href=""#electron""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electron"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electron</span></a> moved across an electric potential difference of one <a href=""#volt""><span title=""See entry on this page at § volt"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">volt</span></a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electronegativity"" title=""Electronegativity"">electronegativity</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A chemical property that describes the tendency of an atom or a functional group to attract electrons (or electron density) towards itself.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electronics"" title=""Electronics"">electronics</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A field that deals with <a href=""#electric_circuit""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electric circuit"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electrical circuits</span></a> that involve active electrical components such as vacuum tubes, transistors, diodes and integrated circuits, and associated passive interconnection technologies.</dd>"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fermion"" title=""Fermion"">fermion</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A type of <a href=""#particle""><span title=""See entry on this page at § particle"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">particle</span></a> that behaves according to <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fermi%E2%80%93Dirac_statistics"" title=""Fermi–Dirac statistics"">Fermi–Dirac statistics</a>, obeys the <a href=""#pauli_exclusion_principle""><span title=""See entry on this page at § Pauli exclusion principle"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">Pauli exclusion principle</span></a>, and possesses half-integer <a href=""#spin""><span title=""See entry on this page at § spin"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">spin</span></a>. Fermions include all <a href=""#quark""><span title=""See entry on this page at § quark"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">quarks</span></a> and <a href=""#lepton""><span title=""See entry on this page at § lepton"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">leptons</span></a>, as well as all <a href=""#composite_particle""><span title=""See entry on this page at § composite particle"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">composite particles</span></a> made of an odd number of these (such as all <a href=""#baryon""><span title=""See entry on this page at § baryon"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">baryons</span></a> and many <a href=""#atom""><span title=""See entry on this page at § atom"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">atoms</span></a> and <a href=""#nucleus""><span title=""See entry on this page at § nucleus"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">nuclei</span></a>). Fermions constitute one of two main classes of particles, the other being <a href=""#boson""><span title=""See entry on this page at § boson"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">bosons</span></a>.</dd>"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Force"" title=""Force"">force</a> (F) (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Any interaction that, when unopposed, will change the <a href=""#motion""><span title=""See entry on this page at § motion"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">motion</span></a> of a physical body. A force has both magnitude and direction, making it a <a href=""#vector""><span title=""See entry on this page at § vector"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">vector</span></a> quantity. The <a href=""#sI""><span title=""See entry on this page at § SI"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">SI</span></a> unit used to measure force is the <a href=""#newton""><span title=""See entry on this page at § newton"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">newton</span></a>.</dd>"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravitation"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Gravitation"">gravitation</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called <b>gravity</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">A natural phenomenon by which physical bodies attract each other with a <a href=""#force""><span title=""See entry on this page at § force"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">force</span></a> proportional to their <a href=""#mass""><span title=""See entry on this page at § mass"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">masses</span></a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravitational_constant"" title=""Gravitational constant"">gravitational constant</a> (G) (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called the <b>universal gravitational constant</b> and <b>Newton's constant</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">A physical constant involved in the calculation of <a href=""#gravitation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § gravitation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">gravitational force</span></a> between two bodies.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravitational_energy"" title=""Gravitational energy"">gravitational energy</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The <a href=""#potential_energy""><span title=""See entry on this page at § potential energy"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">potential energy</span></a> associated with the <a href=""#gravitational_field""><span title=""See entry on this page at § gravitational field"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">gravitational field</span></a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravitational_field"" title=""Gravitational field"">gravitational field</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A model used to explain the influence that a massive body extends into the space around itself, producing a force on another massive body. Thus, a gravitational field is used to explain gravitational phenomena. It is measured in newtons per kilogram (N/kg).</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravitational_potential"" title=""Gravitational potential"">gravitational potential</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The gravitational potential at a location is equal to the <a href=""#work""><span title=""See entry on this page at § work"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">work</span></a> (<a href=""#energy""><span title=""See entry on this page at § energy"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">energy</span></a> transferred) per unit <a href=""#mass""><span title=""See entry on this page at § mass"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">mass</span></a> that is done by the force of <a href=""#gravitation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § gravitation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">gravity</span></a> to move an object to a fixed reference location.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gravitational_wave"" title=""Gravitational wave"">gravitational wave</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A ripple in the curvature of <a href=""#spacetime""><span title=""See entry on this page at § spacetime"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">spacetime</span></a> that propagates as a <a href=""#wave""><span title=""See entry on this page at § wave"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">wave</span></a> and is generated in certain gravitational interactions, travelling outward from their source.</dd>"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joule"" title=""Joule"">joule</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A derived unit of <a href=""#energy""><span title=""See entry on this page at § energy"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">energy</span></a>, <a href=""#work""><span title=""See entry on this page at § work"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">work</span></a>, or amount of heat in the International System of Units.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kelvin"" title=""Kelvin"">Kelvin</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A scale and unit of measurement of <a href=""#temperature""><span title=""See entry on this page at § temperature"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">temperature</span></a>. The Kelvin scale is an absolute <a href=""#thermodynamic_temperature""><span title=""See entry on this page at § thermodynamic temperature"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">thermodynamic temperature</span></a> scale which uses <a href=""#absolute_zero""><span title=""See entry on this page at § absolute zero"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">absolute zero</span></a> as its null point.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kinematics"" title=""Kinematics"">kinematics</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The branch of <a href=""#classical_mechanics""><span title=""See entry on this page at § classical mechanics"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">classical mechanics</span></a> that describes the <a href=""#motion""><span title=""See entry on this page at § motion"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">motion</span></a> of points, bodies (objects) and systems of bodies (groups of objects) without consideration of the causes of motion. The study of kinematics is often referred to as the ""geometry of motion"".</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kinetic_energy"" title=""Kinetic energy"">kinetic energy</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The <a href=""#energy""><span title=""See entry on this page at § energy"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">energy</span></a> that a physical body possesses due to its <a href=""#motion""><span title=""See entry on this page at § motion"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">motion</span></a>, defined as the <a href=""#work""><span title=""See entry on this page at § work"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">work</span></a> needed to <a href=""#acceleration""><span title=""See entry on this page at § acceleration"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">accelerate</span></a> a body of a given <a href=""#mass""><span title=""See entry on this page at § mass"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">mass</span></a> from rest to its stated <a href=""#velocity""><span title=""See entry on this page at § velocity"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">velocity</span></a>. The body continues to maintain this kinetic energy unless its velocity changes. Contrast <i><a href=""#potential_energy""><span title=""See entry on this page at § potential energy"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">potential energy</span></a></i>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kirchhoff%27s_circuit_laws"" title=""Kirchhoff&#39;s circuit laws"">Kirchhoff's circuit laws</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Two approximate equalities that deal with the current and voltage in electrical circuits.also called Kirchhoff's rules or simply Kirchhoff's laws (see also <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kirchhoff%27s_law_of_thermal_radiation"" title=""Kirchhoff&#39;s law of thermal radiation"">Kirchhoff's laws</a> for other meanings of that term).</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kirchhoff%27s_equations"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Kirchhoff&#39;s equations"">Kirchhoff's equations</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">In fluid dynamics, the Kirchhoff equations describe the motion of a rigid body in an ideal fluid.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lagrangian_mechanics"" title=""Lagrangian mechanics"">Lagrangian mechanics</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Laminar_flow"" title=""Laminar flow"">laminar flow</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called <b>streamline flow</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">Occurs when a fluid flows in parallel layers with no disruption between the layers.</dd>"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Laplace%E2%80%93Runge%E2%80%93Lenz_vector"" title=""Laplace–Runge–Lenz vector"">Laplace–Runge–Lenz vector</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also abbreviated <b>LRL vector</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">A <a href=""#vector""><span title=""See entry on this page at § vector"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">vector</span></a> used chiefly to describe the shape and orientation of the orbit of one astronomical body around another, such as a planet revolving around a star. For two bodies interacting by Newtonian gravity, the LRL vector is a constant of motion, meaning that it is the same no matter where it is calculated on the orbit; equivalently, the LRL vector is said to be conserved.</dd>"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lepton"" title=""Lepton"">lepton</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">An elementary particle which does not undergo <a href=""#strong_interaction""><span title=""See entry on this page at § strong interaction"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">strong interactions</span></a> but is subject to the <a href=""#pauli_exclusion_principle""><span title=""See entry on this page at § Pauli exclusion principle"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">Pauli exclusion principle</span></a>. Two main classes of leptons exist: charged leptons (also known as the <a href=""#electron""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electron"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electron</span></a>-like leptons) and neutral leptons (better known as <a href=""#neutrino""><span title=""See entry on this page at § neutrino"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">neutrinos</span></a>).</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lever"" title=""Lever"">lever</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A type of <a href=""#machine""><span title=""See entry on this page at § machine"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">machine</span></a> consisting of a beam or rigid rod pivoted at a fixed hinge or fulcrum; one of six classical <a href=""#simple_machine""><span title=""See entry on this page at § simple machine"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">simple machines</span></a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Light"" title=""Light"">light</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A form of <a href=""#electromagnetic_radiation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electromagnetic radiation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electromagnetic radiation</span></a> that occupies a certain range of <a href=""#wavelength""><span title=""See entry on this page at § wavelength"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">wavelengths</span></a> within the <a href=""#electromagnetic_spectrum""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electromagnetic spectrum"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electromagnetic spectrum</span></a>. In physics, the term sometimes refers collectively to electromagnetic radiation of any wavelength, in which case light includes <a href=""#gamma_ray""><span title=""See entry on this page at § gamma ray"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">gamma rays</span></a>, <a href=""#x-ray""><span title=""See entry on this page at § X-ray"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">X-rays</span></a>, <a href=""#microwave""><span title=""See entry on this page at § microwave"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">microwaves</span></a>, and <a href=""#radio_wave""><span title=""See entry on this page at § radio wave"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">radio waves</span></a>, but in common usage ""light"" more often refers specifically to <a href=""#visible_light""><span title=""See entry on this page at § visible light"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">visible light</span></a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linear_actuator"" title=""Linear actuator"">linear actuator</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A form of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Engine"" title=""Engine"">motor</a> that generates a <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linear"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Linear"">linear</a> movement directly.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linear_algebra"" title=""Linear algebra"">linear algebra</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The branch of <a href=""#mathematics""><span title=""See entry on this page at § mathematics"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">mathematics</span></a> concerning <a href=""#vector_space""><span title=""See entry on this page at § vector space"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">vector spaces</span></a>, often finite or countably infinite dimensional, as well as linear mappings between such spaces.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Line_of_force"" title=""Line of force"">line of force</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p>Looks like this term doesn't have a definition yet.</p>

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Linear_elasticity"" title=""Linear elasticity"">linear elasticity</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The mathematical study of how solid objects deform and become internally stressed due to prescribed loading conditions. Linear elasticity is a simplification of the more general <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Nonlinear_theory_of_elasticity&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1"" class=""new"" title=""Nonlinear theory of elasticity (page does not exist)"">nonlinear theory of elasticity</a> and is a branch of <a href=""#continuum_mechanics""><span title=""See entry on this page at § continuum mechanics"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">continuum mechanics</span></a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liouville%27s_theorem_(conformal_mappings)"" title=""Liouville&#39;s theorem (conformal mappings)"">Liouville's theorem</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Phase space volume is conserved.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liquid"" title=""Liquid"">liquid</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">One of four classical <a href=""#state_of_matter""><span title=""See entry on this page at § state of matter"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">states of matter</span></a> having a definite <a href=""#volume""><span title=""See entry on this page at § volume"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">volume</span></a> but no fixed shape.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Liquid_crystal"" title=""Liquid crystal"">liquid crystal</a> (LC) (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A matter in a state that has properties between those of a conventional liquid and those of a solid crystal. For instance, an LC may flow like a liquid, but its <a href=""#molecule""><span title=""See entry on this page at § molecule"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">molecules</span></a> may be oriented in a crystal-like way.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Longitudinal_wave"" title=""Longitudinal wave"">longitudinal wave</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p>Looks like this term doesn't have a definition yet.</p>

"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/M-theory"" title=""M-theory"">M-theory</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">An extension of <a href=""#string_theory""><span title=""See entry on this page at § string theory"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">string theory</span></a> that attempts to unify seemingly contradictory mathematical formulations and which identifies 11 dimensions.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mach_number"" title=""Mach number"">Mach number</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A dimensionless quantity representing the ratio of the speed of an object moving through a fluid to the local speed of sound.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Machine"" title=""Machine"">machine</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Any powered tool consisting of one or more parts that is constructed to achieve a particular goal. Machines are usually powered by mechanical, chemical, thermal or electrical means, and are frequently motorised.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Machine_element"" title=""Machine element"">machine element</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">An elementary component of a <a href=""#machine""><span title=""See entry on this page at § machine"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">machine</span></a>. There are three basic types: structural components, mechanisms and control components.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maclaurin_series"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Maclaurin series"">Maclaurin series</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A representation of a function as an infinite sum of terms that are calculated from the values of the function's derivatives at a single point.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetic_field"" title=""Magnetic field"">magnetic field</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A mathematical description of the magnetic influence of electric currents and magnetic materials. The magnetic field at any given point is specified by both a direction and a magnitude (or strength); as such it is a <a href=""#vector""><span title=""See entry on this page at § vector"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">vector</span></a> field.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetism"" title=""Magnetism"">magnetism</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A property of materials that respond to an applied <a href=""#magnetic_field""><span title=""See entry on this page at § magnetic field"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">magnetic field</span></a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magnetostatics"" title=""Magnetostatics"">magnetostatics</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p>Looks like this term doesn't have a definition yet.</p>

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mass_balance"" title=""Mass balance"">mass balance</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called <b>material balance</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">An application of the law of <a href=""#conservation_of_mass""><span title=""See entry on this page at § conservation of mass"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">conservation of mass</span></a> to the analysis of physical systems.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mass_density"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Mass density"">mass density</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">See <i><a href=""#density""><span title=""See entry on this page at § density"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">density</span></a></i>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mass_flux"" title=""Mass flux"">mass flux</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The rate of mass flow per unit area. The common symbols are j, J, φ, or Φ, sometimes with subscript m to indicate mass is the flowing quantity. Its SI units are kg s−1 m−2.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mass_moment_of_inertia"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Mass moment of inertia"">mass moment of inertia</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A property of a distribution of mass in space that measures its resistance to rotational acceleration about an axis.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mass_number"" title=""Mass number"">mass number</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called <b>atomic mass number</b> or <b>nucleon number</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">The total number of <a href=""#proton""><span title=""See entry on this page at § proton"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">protons</span></a> and <a href=""#neutron""><span title=""See entry on this page at § neutron"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">neutrons</span></a> (together known as <a href=""#nucleon""><span title=""See entry on this page at § nucleon"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">nucleons</span></a>) in an atomic nucleus.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mass_spectrometry"" title=""Mass spectrometry"">mass spectrometry</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Materials_science"" title=""Materials science"">materials science</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">An interdisciplinary field incorporating elements of physics, chemistry, and engineering that is concerned with the design and discovery of new materials, particularly <a href=""#solid""><span title=""See entry on this page at § solid"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">solids</span></a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_physics"" title=""Mathematical physics"">mathematical physics</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The application of <a href=""#mathematics""><span title=""See entry on this page at § mathematics"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">mathematics</span></a> to problems in physics and the development of mathematical methods suitable for such applications and for the formulation of physical theories.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematics"" title=""Mathematics"">mathematics</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The abstract study of topics encompassing quantity, structure, space, change, and other properties.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Matrix_(mathematics)"" title=""Matrix (mathematics)"">matrix</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A rectangular array of numbers, symbols, or expressions arranged in rows and columns. The individual items in a matrix are called its <i>elements</i> or <i>entries</i>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Matter"" title=""Matter"">matter</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Any substance (often a particle) that has rest mass and (usually) also volume.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Maxwell%27s_equations"" title=""Maxwell&#39;s equations"">Maxwell's equations</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A set of partial differential equations that, together with the Lorentz force law, form the foundation of classical electrodynamics, classical optics, and electric circuits. Maxwell's equations describe how electric and magnetic fields are generated and altered by each other and by charges and currents.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Measure_of_central_tendency"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Measure of central tendency"">measure of central tendency</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A term which relates to the way in which quantitative data tend to cluster around some value. A measure of central tendency is any of a number of ways of specifying this ""central value"".</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mechanical_energy"" title=""Mechanical energy"">mechanical energy</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mechanics"" title=""Mechanics"">mechanics</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The branch of science concerned with the behaviour of physical bodies when subjected to forces or displacements and the subsequent effects of the bodies on their environment.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Melting"" title=""Melting"">melting</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called <b>fusion</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">A physical process that results in the phase transition of a substance from a solid to a liquid.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meson"" title=""Meson"">meson</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A type of <a href=""#hadron""><span title=""See entry on this page at § hadron"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">hadronic</span></a> <a href=""#subatomic_particle""><span title=""See entry on this page at § subatomic particle"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">subatomic particle</span></a> composed of one <a href=""#quark""><span title=""See entry on this page at § quark"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">quark</span></a> and one <a href=""#antiquark""><span title=""See entry on this page at § antiquark"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">antiquark</span></a> bound together by the <a href=""#strong_interaction""><span title=""See entry on this page at § strong interaction"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">strong interaction</span></a>. All mesons are unstable, with the longest-lived lasting for only a few hundredths of a microsecond.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modulus_of_elasticity"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Modulus of elasticity"">modulus of elasticity</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The mathematical description of an object's or substance's tendency to be <a href=""#deformation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § deformation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">deformed</span></a> elastically (i.e., non-permanently) when a force is applied to it. The elastic modulus of an object is defined as the slope of its <a href=""#stress–strain_curve""><span title=""See entry on this page at § stress–strain curve"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">stress–strain curve</span></a> in the elastic deformation region. As such, a <a href=""#stiffness""><span title=""See entry on this page at § stiffness"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">stiffer</span></a> material will have a higher elastic modulus.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molar_concentration"" title=""Molar concentration"">molar concentration</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p>Looks like this term doesn't have a definition yet.</p>

"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molar_mass"" title=""Molar mass"">molar mass</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A physical property of <a href=""#matter""><span title=""See entry on this page at § matter"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">matter</span></a> defined as the <a href=""#mass""><span title=""See entry on this page at § mass"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">mass</span></a> of a given substance divided by the amount of substance and expressed in grams per mole.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molecule"" title=""Molecule"">molecule</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">An electrically neutral group of two or more <a href=""#atom""><span title=""See entry on this page at § atom"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">atoms</span></a> held together by covalent chemical bonds. Molecules are distinguished from <a href=""#ion""><span title=""See entry on this page at § ion"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">ions</span></a> by their lack of electrical charge.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molecular_physics"" title=""Molecular physics"">molecular physics</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A branch of physics that studies the physical properties of <a href=""#molecule""><span title=""See entry on this page at § molecule"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">molecules</span></a> and the chemical bonds between <a href=""#atom""><span title=""See entry on this page at § atom"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">atoms</span></a> as well as their molecular dynamics. It is closely related to atomic physics and overlaps greatly with theoretical chemistry, <a href=""#physical_chemistry""><span title=""See entry on this page at § physical chemistry"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">physical chemistry</span></a> and <a href=""#chemical_physics""><span title=""See entry on this page at § chemical physics"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">chemical physics</span></a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moment_(physics)"" title=""Moment (physics)"">moment</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p>Looks like this term doesn't have a definition yet.</p>

"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moment_of_inertia"" title=""Moment of inertia"">moment of inertia</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A property of a distribution of <a href=""#mass""><span title=""See entry on this page at § mass"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">mass</span></a> in space that measures its resistance to rotational acceleration about an axis.</dd>"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Motion_(physics)"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Motion (physics)"">motion</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Any change in the position of an object over <a href=""#time""><span title=""See entry on this page at § time"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">time</span></a>. Motion can be mathematically described in terms of <a href=""#displacement_(vector)""><span title=""See entry on this page at § displacement (vector)"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">displacement</span></a>, <a href=""#distance""><span title=""See entry on this page at § distance"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">distance</span></a>, <a href=""#velocity""><span title=""See entry on this page at § velocity"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">velocity</span></a>, <a href=""#speed""><span title=""See entry on this page at § speed"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">speed</span></a>, <a href=""#acceleration""><span title=""See entry on this page at § acceleration"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">acceleration</span></a>, and <a href=""#momentum""><span title=""See entry on this page at § momentum"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">momentum</span></a>, and is observed by attaching a <a href=""#frame_of_reference""><span title=""See entry on this page at § frame of reference"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">frame of reference</span></a> to an observer and measuring the change in an object's position relative to that frame. An object's motion cannot change unless it is acted upon by a <a href=""#force""><span title=""See entry on this page at § force"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">force</span></a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Muon"" title=""Muon"">muon</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">An elementary particle, technically classified as a <a href=""#lepton""><span title=""See entry on this page at § lepton"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">lepton</span></a>, that is similar to the <a href=""#electron""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electron"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electron</span></a>, with unitary negative electric charge (−1) and a spin of 1⁄2. Muons are not believed to have any sub-structure.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanoengineering"" title=""Nanoengineering"">nanoengineering</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The practice of engineering on the nanoscale. Nanoengineering is largely a synonym for <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanotechnology"" title=""Nanotechnology"">nanotechnology</a>, but emphasizes the applied rather than the pure science aspects of the field.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nanotechnology"" title=""Nanotechnology"">nanotechnology</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also abbreviated as <b>nanotech</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">The manipulation of matter on an <a href=""#atom""><span title=""See entry on this page at § atom"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">atomic</span></a> and <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molecule"" title=""Molecule"">molecular</a> scale. A more generalized description of nanotechnology is ""the manipulation of matter with at least one dimension sized from 1 to 100 nanometers"".</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Navier%E2%80%93Stokes_equations"" title=""Navier–Stokes equations"">Navier–Stokes equations</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neutrino"" title=""Neutrino"">neutrino</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A type of electrically neutral <a href=""#subatomic_particle""><span title=""See entry on this page at § subatomic particle"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">subatomic particle</span></a> denoted by the Greek letter ν (nu). All evidence suggests that neutrinos have mass but that their mass is tiny even by the standards of subatomic particles. Their mass has never been measured accurately.</dd>"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Newton%27s_laws_of_motion"" title=""Newton&#39;s laws of motion"">Newton's laws of motion</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A set of three physical laws which describe the relationship between the <a href=""#force""><span title=""See entry on this page at § force"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">forces</span></a> acting on a body and its motion due to those forces. Together they form the basis for <a href=""#classical_mechanics""><span title=""See entry on this page at § classical mechanics"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">classical or Newtonian mechanics</span></a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Newton%27s_law_of_universal_gravitation"" title=""Newton&#39;s law of universal gravitation"">Newton's law of universal gravitation</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclide"" title=""Nuclide"">nuclide</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also spelled <b>nucleide</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">An <a href=""#atomic""><span title=""See entry on this page at § atomic"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">atomic</span></a> species characterized by the specific composition of its <a href=""#nucleus""><span title=""See entry on this page at § nucleus"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">nucleus</span></a>, i.e. by its number of <a href=""#proton""><span title=""See entry on this page at § proton"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">protons</span></a>, its number of <a href=""#neutron""><span title=""See entry on this page at § neutron"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">neutrons</span></a>, and its nuclear <a href=""#energy_level""><span title=""See entry on this page at § energy level"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">energy state</span></a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ohm"" title=""Ohm"">Ohm</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The SI derived unit of electrical resistance.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ohm%27s_law"" title=""Ohm&#39;s law"">Ohm's law</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">States that the current through a conductor between two points is directly proportional to the potential difference across the two points.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Optical_tweezers"" title=""Optical tweezers"">optical tweezers</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">An <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Optomechanical"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Optomechanical"">optomechanical</a> device used for the capture, analysis and manipulation of dielectric objects or particles, which operates via the application of force by the electric field of light.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Optically_detected_magnetic_resonance"" title=""Optically detected magnetic resonance"">optically detected magnetic resonance</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">An optical technique for the initialisation and readout of <a href=""#quantum_spin""><span title=""See entry on this page at § quantum spin"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">quantum spin</span></a> in some crystal defects.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Optics"" title=""Optics"">optics</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The branch of physics which involves the behaviour and properties of <a href=""#light""><span title=""See entry on this page at § light"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">light</span></a>, including its interactions with matter and the construction of instruments that use or detect it. Optics usually describes the behaviour of visible, ultraviolet, and infrared light; however, because light is one of several forms of <a href=""#electromagnetic_radiation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electromagnetic radiation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electromagnetic radiation</span></a>, other forms such as <a href=""#x-ray""><span title=""See entry on this page at § X-ray"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">X-rays</span></a>, microwaves, and radio waves exhibit similar properties.</dd>"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Particle_physics"" title=""Particle physics"">particle physics</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A branch of physics that studies the nature of <a href=""#particle""><span title=""See entry on this page at § particle"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">particles</span></a>, which are the constituents of what is usually referred to as <a href=""#matter""><span title=""See entry on this page at § matter"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">matter</span></a> and <a href=""#radiation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § radiation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">radiation</span></a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pascal%27s_law"" title=""Pascal&#39;s law"">Pascal's law</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A principle in <a href=""#fluid_mechanics""><span title=""See entry on this page at § fluid mechanics"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">fluid mechanics</span></a> which states that <a href=""#pressure""><span title=""See entry on this page at § pressure"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">pressure</span></a> exerted anywhere in a confined incompressible fluid is transmitted equally in all directions throughout the fluid such that the initial pressure variations remain the same.</dd>"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physics"" title=""Physics"">physics</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The natural science that involves the study of <a href=""#matter""><span title=""See entry on this page at § matter"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">matter</span></a> and its motion through <a href=""#space""><span title=""See entry on this page at § space"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">space</span></a> and <a href=""#time""><span title=""See entry on this page at § time"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">time</span></a>, along with related concepts such as <a href=""#energy""><span title=""See entry on this page at § energy"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">energy</span></a> and <a href=""#force""><span title=""See entry on this page at § force"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">force</span></a>. More broadly, it is the general analysis of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nature"" title=""Nature"">nature</a>, conducted in order to understand how the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Universe"" title=""Universe"">universe</a> behaves.</dd>"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_electrodynamics"" title=""Quantum electrodynamics"">quantum electrodynamics</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The relativistic <a href=""#quantum_field_theory""><span title=""See entry on this page at § quantum field theory"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">quantum field theory</span></a> of <a href=""#electrodynamics""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electrodynamics"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electrodynamics</span></a>. In essence, it describes how light and matter interact and is the first theory where full agreement between <a href=""#quantum_mechanics""><span title=""See entry on this page at § quantum mechanics"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">quantum mechanics</span></a> and <a href=""#special_relativity""><span title=""See entry on this page at § special relativity"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">special relativity</span></a> is achieved. QED mathematically describes all phenomena involving electrically charged particles interacting by means of exchange of <a href=""#photon""><span title=""See entry on this page at § photon"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">photons</span></a> and represents the quantum counterpart of <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Classical_electromagnetism"" title=""Classical electromagnetism"">classical electromagnetism</a>, giving a complete account of matter and light interaction.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_field_theory"" title=""Quantum field theory"">quantum field theory</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A theoretical framework for constructing quantum mechanical models of <a href=""#subatomic_particle""><span title=""See entry on this page at § subatomic particle"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">subatomic particles</span></a> in <a href=""#particle_physics""><span title=""See entry on this page at § particle physics"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">particle physics</span></a> and <a href=""#quasiparticle""><span title=""See entry on this page at § quasiparticle"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">quasiparticles</span></a> in <a href=""#condensed_matter_physics""><span title=""See entry on this page at § condensed matter physics"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">condensed matter physics</span></a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_gravity"" title=""Quantum gravity"">quantum gravity</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_mechanics"" title=""Quantum mechanics"">quantum mechanics</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A branch of physics dealing with physical phenomena at microscopic scales, where the action is on the order of the <a href=""#planck_constant""><span title=""See entry on this page at § Planck constant"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">Planck constant</span></a>. Quantum mechanics departs from classical mechanics primarily at the <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quantum_realm"" title=""Quantum realm"">quantum realm</a> of <a href=""#atom""><span title=""See entry on this page at § atom"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">atomic</span></a> and <a href=""#subatomic_particle""><span title=""See entry on this page at § subatomic particle"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">subatomic</span></a> length scales. Quantum mechanics provides a mathematical description of much of the dual particle-like and wave-like behavior and interactions of energy and matter.</dd>"
