"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radionuclide"" title=""Radionuclide"">radionuclide</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called a <b>radioactive nuclide</b>, <b>radioisotope</b>, or <b>radioactive isotope</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">Any <a href=""#nuclide""><span title=""See entry on this page at § nuclide"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">nuclide</span></a> possessing excess nuclear energy to the point that it is unstable. Such excess energy is emitted through any of several processes of <a href=""#radioactive_decay""><span title=""See entry on this page at § radioactive decay"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">radioactive decay</span></a>, resulting in a <a href=""#stable_nuclide""><span title=""See entry on this page at § stable nuclide"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">stable nuclide</span></a> or sometimes another unstable radionuclide which can then undergo further decay. Certain radionuclides can occur naturally; many others can be produced artificially in <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nuclear_reactor"" title=""Nuclear reactor"">nuclear reactors</a>, <a href=""#cyclotron""><span title=""See entry on this page at § cyclotron"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">cyclotrons</span></a>, <a href=""#particle_accelerator""><span title=""See entry on this page at § particle accelerator"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">particle accelerators</span></a>, or radionuclide generators.</dd>"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Redshift"" title=""Redshift"">redshift</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A phenomenon which occurs when <a href=""#light""><span title=""See entry on this page at § light"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">light</span></a> seen coming from an object that is moving away is proportionally increased in <a href=""#wavelength""><span title=""See entry on this page at § wavelength"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">wavelength</span></a> or ""shifted"" to the red end of the <a href=""#visible_light""><span title=""See entry on this page at § visible light"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">visible light</span></a> spectrum.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Refraction"" title=""Refraction"">refraction</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The change in direction of a <a href=""#wave""><span title=""See entry on this page at § wave"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">wave</span></a> as it passes from one <a href=""#transmission_medium""><span title=""See entry on this page at § transmission medium"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">transmission medium</span></a> to another or as a result of a gradual change in the medium. Though most commonly used in the context of refraction of <a href=""#light""><span title=""See entry on this page at § light"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">light</span></a>, other waves such as <a href=""#sound""><span title=""See entry on this page at § sound"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">sound</span></a> waves and <a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wind_wave"" title=""Wind wave"">fluid waves</a> also experience refraction.</dd>"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rigid_body"" title=""Rigid body"">rigid body</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">An idealization of a solid body in which <a href=""#deformation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § deformation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">deformation</span></a> is neglected. In other words, the distance between any two given points of a rigid body remains constant in time regardless of the external forces exerted on it. Even though such an object cannot physically exist due to relativity, objects can normally be assumed to be perfectly rigid if they are not moving near the <a href=""#speed_of_light""><span title=""See entry on this page at § speed of light"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">speed of light</span></a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rotational_energy"" title=""Rotational energy"">rotational energy</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called <b>angular kinetic energy</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">The <a href=""#kinetic_energy""><span title=""See entry on this page at § kinetic energy"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">kinetic energy</span></a> due to the rotation of an object and forms part of its total kinetic energy.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rotational_speed"" title=""Rotational speed"">rotational speed</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called <b>speed of revolution</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">The number of complete rotations or revolutions a rotating body makes per unit time.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rydberg_formula"" title=""Rydberg formula"">Rydberg formula</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A formula used in atomic physics to describe the wavelengths of spectral lines of many chemical elements.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scalar_(physics)"" title=""Scalar (physics)"">scalar</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">Any simple physical quantity that can be described by a single number (as opposed to <a href=""#vector""><span title=""See entry on this page at § vector"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">vectors</span></a>, <a href=""#tensor""><span title=""See entry on this page at § tensor"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">tensors</span></a>, etc., which are described by several numbers such as magnitude and direction) and is unchanged by coordinate system rotations or translations (in Newtonian mechanics) or by Lorentz transformations or central-time translations (in relativity).</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Scattering"" title=""Scattering"">scattering</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The general physical process by which some forms of radiation, such as light, sound, or moving particles, are forced to deviate from a straight <a href=""#trajectory""><span title=""See entry on this page at § trajectory"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">trajectory</span></a> by one or more localised non-uniformities in the medium through which they pass.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Science"" title=""Science"">science</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A systematic enterprise that builds and organises knowledge in the form of testable explanations and predictions about the universe.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Screw_(simple_machine)"" title=""Screw (simple machine)"">screw</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A mechanism that converts rotational motion to linear motion, and a <a href=""#torque""><span title=""See entry on this page at § torque"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">torque</span></a> (rotational force) to a linear force; one of six classical <a href=""#simple_machine""><span title=""See entry on this page at § simple machine"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">simple machines</span></a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second_law_of_thermodynamics"" title=""Second law of thermodynamics"">second law of thermodynamics</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"

<dd class=""glossary"">A mechanical device that changes the direction or magnitude of a <a href=""#force""><span title=""See entry on this page at § force"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">force</span></a>. <dd class=""glossary"">A tube in an inverted U shape that causes a liquid to flow uphill without pumps, powered by the fall of the liquid as it flows down the tube under the pull of <a href=""#gravity""><span title=""See entry on this page at § gravity"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">gravity</span></a>. The term may also more generally refer to a wide variety of devices involving the flow of liquids through tubes.</dd>"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Solubility"" title=""Solubility"">solubility</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The tendency of a solid, liquid, or gaseous chemical substance (called a <i>solute</i>) to dissolve in another solid, liquid, or gaseous substance (called a <i>solvent</i>) to form a homogeneous solution of the solute in the solvent. The solubility of a solute fundamentally depends on the specific solvent as well as on <a href=""#temperature""><span title=""See entry on this page at § temperature"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">temperature</span></a> and <a href=""#pressure""><span title=""See entry on this page at § pressure"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">pressure</span></a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sound"" title=""Sound"">sound</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A mechanical <a href=""#wave""><span title=""See entry on this page at § wave"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">wave</span></a> that is an oscillation of <a href=""#pressure""><span title=""See entry on this page at § pressure"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">pressure</span></a> transmitted through a solid, liquid, or gas and composed of frequencies within the range of human hearing.</dd>"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speed_of_light"" title=""Speed of light"">speed of light</a> (c) (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"

When not otherwise qualified, the term ""speed of light"" usually refers to the speed of light in a <a href=""#vacuum""><span title=""See entry on this page at § vacuum"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">vacuum</span></a> as opposed to the speed of light through some physical medium.</dd>"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Statics"" title=""Statics"">statics</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The branch of mechanics concerned with the analysis of loads (<a href=""#force""><span title=""See entry on this page at § force"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">force</span></a> and <a href=""#torque""><span title=""See entry on this page at § torque"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">torque</span></a>, or ""moment"") on physical systems in static equilibrium, that is, in a state where the relative positions of subsystems do not vary over time, or where components and structures are at a constant <a href=""#velocity""><span title=""See entry on this page at § velocity"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">velocity</span></a>.</dd>"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stress_(mechanics)"" title=""Stress (mechanics)"">stress</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">1.&#160;&#160;An applied <a href=""#force""><span title=""See entry on this page at § force"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">force</span></a> or system of forces that tends to <a href=""#strain""><span title=""See entry on this page at § strain"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">strain</span></a> or <a href=""#deformation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § deformation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">deform</span></a> a physical body.</dd>
<dd class=""glossary"">2.&#160;&#160;A measure of the internal forces acting within a deformable body.</dd>

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thermal_equilibrium"" title=""Thermal equilibrium"">thermal equilibrium</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A state in which there is no net flow of <a href=""#heat""><span title=""See entry on this page at § heat"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">thermal energy</span></a> between two physical systems when the systems are connected by a path permeable to heat. A system may also be said to be in thermal equilibrium with itself if the <a href=""#temperature""><span title=""See entry on this page at § temperature"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">temperature</span></a> within the system is spatially and temporally uniform. Systems in <a href=""#thermodynamic_equilibrium""><span title=""See entry on this page at § thermodynamic equilibrium"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">thermodynamic equilibrium</span></a> are always in thermal equilibrium, but the converse is not always true.</dd>"

"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Torque"" title=""Torque"">torque</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called <b>moment</b> or <b>moment of force</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">The tendency of a <a href=""#force""><span title=""See entry on this page at § force"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">force</span></a> to rotate an object about an axis, fulcrum, or pivot. "<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Toughness"" title=""Toughness"">toughness</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The ability of a material to absorb <a href=""#energy""><span title=""See entry on this page at § energy"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">energy</span></a> and plastically deform without fracturing. Material toughness is defined as the amount of energy per unit volume that a material can absorb before rupturing. It is also defined as the resistance to fracture of a material when <a href=""#stress""><span title=""See entry on this page at § stress"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">stressed</span></a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trajectory"" title=""Trajectory"">trajectory</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The path that a moving object follows through <a href=""#space""><span title=""See entry on this page at § space"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">space</span></a> as a function of <a href=""#time""><span title=""See entry on this page at § time"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">time</span></a>.</dd>"

<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called the <b>weak force</b> or <b>weak nuclear force</b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">One of the four fundamental forces of nature, along with the <a href=""#strong_nuclear_force""><span title=""See entry on this page at § strong nuclear force"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">strong nuclear force</span></a>, <a href=""#electromagnetism""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electromagnetism"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electromagnetism</span></a>, and <a href=""#gravitation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § gravitation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">gravitation</span></a>. It is responsible for the <a href=""#radioactive_decay""><span title=""See entry on this page at § radioactive decay"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">radioactive decay</span></a> of <a href=""#subatomic_particle""><span title=""See entry on this page at § subatomic particle"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">subatomic particles</span></a> and initiates the process known as <a href=""#fusion""><span title=""See entry on this page at § fusion"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">hydrogen fusion</span></a> in stars.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Weber_(unit)"" title=""Weber (unit)"">weber</a> (Wb) (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"

<dd class=""glossary"">A triangular round tool in the form a compound and portable inclined plane; one of six classical <a href=""#simple_machine""><span title=""See entry on this page at § simple machine"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">simple machines</span></a>.</dd>"

"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wheel_and_axle"" title=""Wheel and axle"">wheel and axle</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A wheel attached to an axle so that these two parts rotate together in which a force is transferred from one to the other; one of six classical <a href=""#simple_machine""><span title=""See entry on this page at § simple machine"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">simple machines</span></a>.</dd>"
"white body (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A hypothetical idealized physical body that reflects all incident <a href=""#electromagnetic_radiation""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electromagnetic radiation"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">electromagnetic radiation</span></a> completely and uniformly in all directions; the opposite of a <a href=""#black_body""><span title=""See entry on this page at § black body"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">black body</span></a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wind"" title=""Wind"">wind</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The flow of gases on a large scale.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wind_shear"" title=""Wind shear"">wind shear</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<p class=""glossary-hatnote""><span role=""note"" class=""hatnote navigation-not-searchable selfref"">Also called <b><a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wind_gradient"" title=""Wind gradient"">wind gradient</a></b>.</span></p>
<dd class=""glossary"">A difference in wind speed and direction over a relatively short distance in the atmosphere. Wind shear can be broken down into vertical and horizontal components, with horizontal wind shear seen across fronts and near the coast, and vertical shear typically near the surface, though also at higher levels in the atmosphere.</dd>"

"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/X-ray"" title=""X-ray"">X-ray</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A high-energy <a href=""#photon""><span title=""See entry on this page at § photon"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">photon</span></a> (between 100 <a href=""#electron_volt""><span title=""See entry on this page at § electron volt"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">eV</span></a> and 100 keV) with a wavelength shorter than that of ultraviolet radiation and longer than that of gamma radiation.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Young%E2%80%99s_modulus"" class=""mw-redirect"" title=""Young’s modulus"">Young’s modulus</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">A measure of the <a href=""#stiffness""><span title=""See entry on this page at § stiffness"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">stiffness</span></a> of a solid material which defines the relationship between mechanical <a href=""#stress""><span title=""See entry on this page at § stress"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">stress</span></a> and <a href=""#strain""><span title=""See entry on this page at § strain"" class=""glossary-link-internal"" style=""border-bottom: 1px dashed #86a1ff; color:initial;"" id="""">strain</span></a>.</dd>"
"<a href=""https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zeeman_effect"" title=""Zeeman effect"">Zeeman effect</a> (Wikipedia Glossary of Physics)"|"
<dd class=""glossary"">The effect of splitting a spectral line into several components in the presence of a static magnetic field by the lifting of degeneracy in electronic states.</dd>"
